<div id="review_59848752" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/59848752" />
    <a title="Hannah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2400165-hannah"><img alt="Hannah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1512687352p2/2400165.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/59848752?book_show_action=true">May 25, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hannah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hannah" href="/user/show/2400165-hannah">Hannah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer59848752" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16967979688858807115">If you read only one book in the Space Trilogy, make it this one. In this one, the "space" travel involves eldils (angels) descending to earth, rather than men traveling to the heavens. It's a high-suspense story (borderline horror, at times) that slowly reveals the evil behind the sterile world of secular scientists and academics pursuing a "a better human race". It's also Lewis at his best in terms of showcasing his grasp of human nature, uncovering the pettiness and self-deception behind the </span>
  <span id="freeText16967979688858807115" style="display:none">If you read only one book in the Space Trilogy, make it this one. In this one, the "space" travel involves eldils (angels) descending to earth, rather than men traveling to the heavens. It's a high-suspense story (borderline horror, at times) that slowly reveals the evil behind the sterile world of secular scientists and academics pursuing a "a better human race". It's also Lewis at his best in terms of showcasing his grasp of human nature, uncovering the pettiness and self-deception behind the striving to belong to the "inner ring" and to prove oneself grown-up and respectable—even within marriage, and even at the expense of friendships and the simple pleasure in life. <br /><br />The whole last quarter of the book is a sort of slow-building, controlled chaos that culminates in the waking of Merlin, the judgement of Babel, the freeing of prisoners (both human and animal), the descent of Mars and Venus to earth, the opening of the pit of hell to swallow up evil, and the restoration of love between husband and wife. It is, as the kids say, <i>epic</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16967979688858807115" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3401709728" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3401709728" />
    <a title="Wreade1872" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7708524-wreade1872"><img alt="Wreade1872" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1606239671p2/7708524.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3401709728?book_show_action=true">Jul 16, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Wreade1872" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Wreade1872" href="/user/show/7708524-wreade1872">Wreade1872</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780020869207" href="/book/show/979598.That_Hideous_Strength">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7708524-wreade1872?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7708524-wreade1872?shelf=1940s">1940s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7708524-wreade1872?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7708524-wreade1872?shelf=dystopian">dystopian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7708524-wreade1872?shelf=supernatural">supernatural</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3401709728" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17636092570919763707"><b>"...and great words like castles came out of his mouth."</b><br/><br/>There were several times i came near deducting marks here. The main characters didn't really do anything in the story. There's a big exposition dump in the epilogue which feels like it should have been in the prologue. Half of it is hilarious and i don't think its meant to be and then there's the female question which we'll get to. <br/> <br/>Nevertheless, this is some of the best writing i've seen from Lewis. Filled with memorable characters and gre</span>
  <span id="freeText17636092570919763707" style="display:none"><b>"...and great words like castles came out of his mouth."</b><br /><br />There were several times i came near deducting marks here. The main characters didn't really do anything in the story. There's a big exposition dump in the epilogue which feels like it should have been in the prologue. Half of it is hilarious and i don't think its meant to be and then there's the female question which we'll get to. <br /> <br />Nevertheless, this is some of the best writing i've seen from Lewis. Filled with memorable characters and great descriptions.<br /><br />Its quite hard to describe because it feels like a clash of many different genres. Most of this comes down to the bad guys. <br />At first i found them very scary, some seemed Kafka-esque others Orwellian, then you find that the group consists of every type of evil trope. The corporate evil, politically evil, religious evil, police state, mad scientists etc. Etc. <br />This is where any sense of horror collapses. There’s just too many genres of bad guy all in one place so that it becomes absurd rather than scary. <br />Each bad guy is also convinced that their plan is the real master plan and they’re just using the others to get to their particular goal. After the third or fourth time you run into yet another new evil scheme, it loses its punch :lol .<br />Any sense of suspense isn’t helped by the evil plans their hatching not even getting off the drawing board. By about half-way it had become a complete farce but a highly entertaining one :) .<br /><br />But i can’t avoid the female elephant in the room. So what seems at first like the main character is a woman and a very well drawn one. In a loveless marriage, intelligent, independent and certainly with ambitions beyond being a housewife or mother. <br />However Lewis only spends so much time crafting this character so that what he says next cannot be dismissed as mere ignorance, he then goes on to state his religious beliefs.<br /><br />Which boil down to, god is a big phallus in the sky and he only gave women legs so they could walk from the bedroom to the kitchen.<br />Lewis expresses this very politely and almost apologetically, after all it isn’t his fault god is like this, it just is... pardon me a moment while i vomit. <br />Anyway, obviously i have not rated this book on its morality. <br /><br />Far more in everyway than the previous two oddities in this very odd trilogy.<br /><br /><b>"Your husband is waiting for you... You will have no more dreams. Have children instead."</b><br /><br />The above quote in context does not mean what it sounds like, but on a subtext level it means exactly what it sounds like :| .</span>
  <a data-text-id="17636092570919763707" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1584676821" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1584676821" />
    <a title="Laura" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/597746-laura"><img alt="Laura" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599928514p2/597746.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1584676821?book_show_action=true">Sep 13, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura" href="/user/show/597746-laura">Laura</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780743234924" href="/book/show/102554.That_Hideous_Strength">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1584676821" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1219808262499077439">I read this the way I've read almost all of C.S. Lewis' writing: first, by sheer determination even though it makes only a little sense to me; then re-reading a second time with appreciation. I almost always start his books, admire his ideas but realize I'm pretty puzzled by most of it, and then go back and re-read the book and realize how brilliant it really is.<br/><br/>Even though it is the last book in his trilogy, as a non-fantasy reader I think I should have started here. This is the least fantastic</span>
  <span id="freeText1219808262499077439" style="display:none">I read this the way I've read almost all of C.S. Lewis' writing: first, by sheer determination even though it makes only a little sense to me; then re-reading a second time with appreciation. I almost always start his books, admire his ideas but realize I'm pretty puzzled by most of it, and then go back and re-read the book and realize how brilliant it really is.<br /><br />Even though it is the last book in his trilogy, as a non-fantasy reader I think I should have started here. This is the least fantastic book of the trilogy and contains the most recognizable setting (a modern university full of intellectuals who value objectivity, embrace materialism, and worship science) and features the most relatable characters (like Jane! Finally a girl I can relate to!)<br /><br />Set in a fictional British university, we first meet Mark Studdock--a young professor trying to climb the ranks of academia not by his knowledge or skill, but by getting chummy with the 'in' crowd. Unbeknownst to him, the in-crowd has recently aligned themselves with an organization called N.I.C.E., an acronym which disguises their real intentions to apply science to all social problems and to sterilize the planet and re-educate people in order to promote the all-powerful human above the disorder of nature. Meanwhile, his wife has been experiencing--rather against her will--prophetic dreams that lead her towards a group of people who are opposed to N.I.C.E. and all that it stands for. Mark and Jane clearly symbolize the objective approach and the subjective approach, and their marriage represents the necessary unity between these two approaches that makes us human. I'm not going to pretend I got all the symbolism in this book (that is something I'd love to keep discussing with other fans of Lewis' work!) but I did recognize the way this story fleshes out ideas found in The Abolition of Man. I doubt anyone could fully appreciate this novel without at least some knowledge of Lewis' argument in The Abolition of Man and his essay on the Inner Ring <a href="https://www.goodreads.com" rel="nofollow noopener">http://www.lewissociety.org/innerring.php</a> though I do think the story stands on its own merits. I'm sure there is hardly a thesis about this book that hasn't already been written, but this book begs to have an essay or two written about it.<br /><br />Using my Lewis formula (read, then re-read), I think I ought to go back and re-read the whole trilogy again so I can experience the brilliance. It's a good thing all of his works deserve a second reading anyhow.<br /><br />(Why 3 stars? I've decided the stars are there for me, to determine how much I enjoyed a book, not to reflect how brilliant the book actually is. Purely subjective. Hence, a three.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="1219808262499077439" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_194331183" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/194331183" />
    <a title="Jacob Aitken" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5958504-jacob-aitken"><img alt="Jacob Aitken" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1463699884p2/5958504.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/194331183?book_show_action=true">Aug 04, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jacob Aitken" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jacob Aitken" href="/user/show/5958504-jacob-aitken">Jacob Aitken</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780684823850" href="/book/show/485873.That_Hideous_Strength">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer194331183" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6849516500899533770">This is easily human literature's finest hour. CS Lewis, in what is easily his masterpiece, gets in one's face about the reality of the New World Order and of the possibilities of real, effective Christian resistance to it. <br/><br/>But the true evil is not democracy. It is diabolical, to be sure, and monarchy is definitely to be preferred, but the true battle takes place on "the unseen world." <br/><br/>Lewis puts "spiritual warfare" in a rather direct, most uncomfortable light. Christians piously prat about spir</span>
  <span id="freeText6849516500899533770" style="display:none">This is easily human literature's finest hour. CS Lewis, in what is easily his masterpiece, gets in one's face about the reality of the New World Order and of the possibilities of real, effective Christian resistance to it. <br /><br />But the true evil is not democracy. It is diabolical, to be sure, and monarchy is definitely to be preferred, but the true battle takes place on "the unseen world." <br /><br />Lewis puts "spiritual warfare" in a rather direct, most uncomfortable light. Christians piously prat about spiritual warfare, but most believe deep down in their hearts that demons aren't real and aerial beings really don't inhabit our world. Lewis destroys that notion.<br /><br />Lewis’ interweaving of Merlin and the “eldils” is perhaps more brilliant than Lewis himself realized.<br /><br />We Christians believe in angels because the Bible says so. But do we really? I think Lewis is letting on a little more than most people realise. Lewis is a medievalist and despite so-called advances in science and technology, he retained much of the old cosmology (cf chapter 3 in *Studies in Medieval and Renaissance Literature*).<br /><br />Most Christians until the Enlightenment (a period by no means friendly to Christianity) believed in beings who weren’t human but also weren’t divine, yet were quite active in the world. One british scholar has pointed out how perfectly-orthodox Irish theologians were able to posit such beings while maintaining a rigorous monotheism.<br /><br />The piercing of the human psyches in this novel is stunning. Words fail me at this moment. Imagine every most incredible adjective, and that is what the book is like.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6849516500899533770" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_75533929" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/75533929" />
    <a title="Brandy Painter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2840210-brandy-painter"><img alt="Brandy Painter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1398019139p2/2840210.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/75533929?book_show_action=true">Oct 23, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brandy Painter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brandy Painter" href="/user/show/2840210-brandy-painter">Brandy Painter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780743234924" href="/book/show/102554.That_Hideous_Strength">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2840210-brandy-painter?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2840210-brandy-painter?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer75533929" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9464568528201581058">Brilliant! Brilliant! Brilliant! How many times can I use that word or one of its synonyms in describing anything written by C.S. Lewis? Not enough. This book, the third in the Space Trilogy, is the best of the three.<br/><br/>That Hideous Strength deals with a Britain on the verge of dystopia. An organization known as the N.I.C.E. is moving to take over the nation and its strength will usher in the hideousness referred to in the title. Like in most dystopian novels there is a small group of individuals w</span>
  <span id="freeText9464568528201581058" style="display:none">Brilliant! Brilliant! Brilliant! How many times can I use that word or one of its synonyms in describing anything written by C.S. Lewis? Not enough. This book, the third in the Space Trilogy, is the best of the three.<br /><br />That Hideous Strength deals with a Britain on the verge of dystopia. An organization known as the N.I.C.E. is moving to take over the nation and its strength will usher in the hideousness referred to in the title. Like in most dystopian novels there is a small group of individuals who see the danger and is fighting against it. However, unlike its counterparts in other novels, this group does not rely on the strength of their individual wills and merits to fight the evil machinery of government. Their strength is in a higher power and they are fully aware that their struggle, while important, is only a small part of the greater battle raging in the Universe around them. They are humble in the realization that they are at most minor players and observers in a battle that is not their own to win or lose. Hope for the future is a solid reality in the plot of this novel and not some nebulous feeling or nonexistent force as it is in most dystopian novels. And woven into all of this is the legend of Arthur Pendragon and Merlin. Modern British fantasy meets ancient British mythology and the result is spectacular. <br /><br />Into the plot C.S. Lewis has injected the themes and philosophies he explores in his non-fiction works The Abolition of Man and The Four Loves. As is typical for his fictional works this aspect does not overshadow the beauty of the story he is telling. It is not a lesson he is teaching but is the story itself. Genius. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9464568528201581058" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3532201" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3532201" />
    <a title="Emilia P" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/76042-emilia-p"><img alt="Emilia P" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1417628630p2/76042.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3532201?book_show_action=true">Jul 25, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emilia P" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emilia P" href="/user/show/76042-emilia-p">Emilia P</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everybody and their mom</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/76042-emilia-p?shelf=real-books">real-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/76042-emilia-p?shelf=churrrch">churrrch</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3532201" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2897972725763162023">That Hideous Strength is the final book in the C.S. Lewis's Ransom trilogy. The first two books find Ransom on Mars and then Venus, exploring their flora and fauna,meeting their inhabitants and speaking with their eldils, which are somewhere between the planets spiritual essence and its guardian angel..while we discover how (the Christian) God works on other planets.<br/><br/>This third book finds Ransom back on earth, preparing for an interplanetary response to the threat of apocalypse, which is about to</span>
  <span id="freeText2897972725763162023" style="display:none">That Hideous Strength is the final book in the C.S. Lewis's Ransom trilogy. The first two books find Ransom on Mars and then Venus, exploring their flora and fauna,meeting their inhabitants and speaking with their eldils, which are somewhere between the planets spiritual essence and its guardian angel..while we discover how (the Christian) God works on other planets.<br /><br />This third book finds Ransom back on earth, preparing for an interplanetary response to the threat of apocalypse, which is about to come at the hands of overeager scientists and academics who are doing the devils work, some knowingly and some because of their own ignorance and disconnectedness from the real world. They are a pretty nasty bunch. Eventually, albeit briefly, he gets Merlin on his side and uhmmm... calls down the gods (from their corresponding planets). Merlin and the gods as ancient magic are neutral, so its okay that the Christian heroes co-opt them. Which, needless to say, I love, since it takes a big man to really celebrate the reality of non -Christian elements being important in the Christian tradition.<br /><br />I loved this book. C.S. Lewis is at his best, blending the everyday human experience with the huge huge questions of existence and god. You know he's being a little didactic, but he sets up his characters so you really feel like its something that they are learning too. Mostly I like this series because its about how God is so much bigger and has so much more work to do than just our human stuff, but we still have a duty to him. Three cheers. Phew.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2897972725763162023" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3201321698" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13727113822702861833">Yay! And with that, I've reached my goal for 2020 in regards to number of books read. But I've only just started my actual goal which is to read through all of C.S. Lewis's published works. I think I'm about a quarter of the way through that part of my goal. <br/><br/>Anyways, this book. I can honestly say this is the first C.S. Lewis book after which hitting about 2/3 of the way in, I really wished I didn't have to read. <br/><br/>I greatly appreciate the concepts and sheer brilliance behind his writing here. The </span>
  <span id="freeText13727113822702861833" style="display:none">Yay! And with that, I've reached my goal for 2020 in regards to number of books read. But I've only just started my actual goal which is to read through all of C.S. Lewis's published works. I think I'm about a quarter of the way through that part of my goal. <br /><br />Anyways, this book. I can honestly say this is the first C.S. Lewis book after which hitting about 2/3 of the way in, I really wished I didn't have to read. <br /><br />I greatly appreciate the concepts and sheer brilliance behind his writing here. The allegorical pieces were so interesting (I don't think I've ever seen anyone else do the Tower of Babel). But there was a lot of grind to get to the rush. Calling this a slow burn would be minimalist at best. First there's a new world order and closed door meetings and a bear and Merlin and a marriage that's on the rocks. And it's mostly shown through a lot. Of. Heavy. Dialogue. The incongruency of this novel to the first two baffled me. It was so much longer and there was nothing of space travel, other worldly creatures, or sinister battles for the soul of [insert planet here]. <br /><br />As with all of C.S. Lewis's works, the book is chock full of diamonds. But I felt like I had to mine through a lot more rough with this book to find them. <br /><br />I'd rate this book a PG-13 for very mild swearing, mild sexual references, and gore.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11395406350618785038"><i>You have done what was required of you. You have obeyed and waited. It will often happen like that.</i><br/><br/>It took me a hundred pages to connect. And I don't love it like my friend who told me today she reads it once a year. I often debate myself (in the midst of reading a book). Is this a 4 star or 5 star book?   <span id="freeText11395406350618785038" style="display:none"><i>You have done what was required of you. You have obeyed and waited. It will often happen like that.</i><br /><br />It took me a hundred pages to connect. And I don't love it like my friend who told me today she reads it once a year. I often debate myself (in the midst of reading a book). Is this a 4 star or 5 star book? I was convinced it was a 4 star book ... but after I finished it, I believe it deserves 5 stars. <br /><br />That Hideous Strength made me think of a plenitude of other works: Jeeves and Wooster; Harry Potter; The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe; King Arthur; Shakespeare; Silence; Jabberwocky; Lord of the Rings; the music of Vaughan Williams.<br /><br />The phrases! <br /><i>all the old women of both sexes<br />What to do if all the Die-Hards one day all refused to do any Die-Harding?<br />the swift efficiency of an angry woman<br />get on or get out<br />It was, no doubt, a "rum do" — the rummiest do that had ever befallen him. <br />the grammar of virtue</i><br /><br />This book is about the desire to be in the inner ring. To what lengths will a person go to be included and accepted? <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11395406350618785038" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2052865476" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2052865476" />
    <a title="Mike Fendrich" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/63603088-mike-fendrich"><img alt="Mike Fendrich" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572470726p2/63603088.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2052865476?book_show_action=true">Jul 25, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike Fendrich" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike Fendrich" href="/user/show/63603088-mike-fendrich">Mike Fendrich</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2052865476" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10574679525217720922">This is probably my 3rd or 4th time reading this book. If Lewis understood anything, he understood the arid desert of modernity and its absolutely crippling effect on what it means to be human (among many, many, many!! other things). The most fascinating theme of the book is the opening of Mark and Jane Studdock's eyes to a life beyond abstraction and "complete objectivity", (reason alone). Can't recommend it highly enough.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3237811478" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3237811478" />
    <a title="Anne Gazzolo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3066514-anne-gazzolo"><img alt="Anne Gazzolo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1370728331p2/3066514.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3237811478?book_show_action=true">Mar 19, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anne Gazzolo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anne Gazzolo" href="/user/show/3066514-anne-gazzolo">Anne Gazzolo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3237811478" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12269909202363308235"><br/>Newly married Jane Studdock is troubled by nightmares she later learns are actual events and is a gift (or curse) powers around her wish to use for good or ill. Her husband, Mark, is a professor at Bracton, an English college more than 700 hundred years old. He desperately wishes to be considered part of the Progressive Element at the school.<br/><br/>Bragdon Wood on the school’s property is even older than it, with ties to the time of Merlin. N.I.C.E. wishes to buy the Wood for their headquarters. Even t</span>
  <span id="freeText12269909202363308235" style="display:none"><br />Newly married Jane Studdock is troubled by nightmares she later learns are actual events and is a gift (or curse) powers around her wish to use for good or ill. Her husband, Mark, is a professor at Bracton, an English college more than 700 hundred years old. He desperately wishes to be considered part of the Progressive Element at the school.<br /><br />Bragdon Wood on the school’s property is even older than it, with ties to the time of Merlin. N.I.C.E. wishes to buy the Wood for their headquarters. Even though I have only read this classic for the first time a couple years ago, I already knew N.I.C.E. was not nice. <br /><br />Mark attends the meeting that decides upon the school's sale of the Wood to N.I.C.E, while Jane lunches with Mr. and Mrs. Drimble. Mr. Drimble was Jane’s tutor in school and an expert on all things Arthurian. Jane’s strange dream intrigues the man. That night, Mark attends a dinner where he hears of N.I.C.E.’s vision of the future, which is truly frightening to anyone with sense (and unfortunately still alive and well today in the real world). But Mark readily agrees to help the organization implement their plans and so begins his slow slide into hell.<br /><br />Jane and Mark are soon caught up in a great battle between light and darkness and on opposite sides. Who will prevail?<br /><br />I liked this book overall. It is chilling how accurate it still is, more than 70 years later, about how the so-called Progressive Element operates with their manipulation of the media to misinform the public, to organize protests and rallies that seem real but are completely staged, and to incite hate and rage. Their goals to eliminate undesirable elements of the human race are right out in the open here and now. Their work toward making man immortal is diabolical. For that alone, it must be read. Let us heed the warnings he gives! <br /><br />It gets rather too strange shortly after the appearance of Merlin (though not because of his appearance). Before then, the strength of Lewis’ writing shows through. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12269909202363308235" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1284476113" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1284476113" />
    <a title="Megan Baxter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5742086-megan-baxter"><img alt="Megan Baxter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1310153693p2/5742086.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1284476113?book_show_action=true">May 19, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Megan Baxter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Megan Baxter" href="/user/show/5742086-megan-baxter">Megan Baxter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1284476113" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2528851659140688282">I started this book with a certain amount of trepidation. I'd liked the first book in this series a lot, but while I had been enchanted with the descriptions of Venus/Perelandra, I was frustrated by the outcome of the second book as a whole. Which way was this going to go? Well, somewhere in the middle. In general, the book was entertaining to read, with occasional passages that made me stop and take a deep breath. It's one of those things where some of the things he says sound fantastic, and th</span>
  <span id="freeText2528851659140688282" style="display:none">I started this book with a certain amount of trepidation. I'd liked the first book in this series a lot, but while I had been enchanted with the descriptions of Venus/Perelandra, I was frustrated by the outcome of the second book as a whole. Which way was this going to go? Well, somewhere in the middle. In general, the book was entertaining to read, with occasional passages that made me stop and take a deep breath. It's one of those things where some of the things he says sound fantastic, and then he says something else frustrating, and I just have to wait for a minute for that to pass through me before I can press onward.<br /><br />Note: The rest of this review has been withdrawn due to the changes in Goodreads policy and enforcement. You can read why I came to this decision <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/story/show/349383-goodreads-this-is-not-okay?chapter=1" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>.<br /><br />In the meantime, you can read the entire review at <a href="http://smorgasbook.blogspot.ca/2015/06/that-hideous-strength-by-cs-lewis.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Smorgasbook</a></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6315422955445289161">Hands down my favorite of his space trilogy books. I absolutely love who Ransom has become as a character and I love that it is set on earth. And shows the real struggle that man has. <br/><br/>I think that this is incredibly creative.   <span id="freeText6315422955445289161" style="display:none">Hands down my favorite of his space trilogy books. I absolutely love who Ransom has become as a character and I love that it is set on earth. And shows the real struggle that man has. <br /><br />I think that this is incredibly creative.   function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked

    }
  }

  }

//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_1800794'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1800794.Planet_Narnia?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Planet Narnia: The Seven Heavens in the Imagination of C.S. Lewis<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/5816302.Michael_Ward?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Michael  Ward<\/a><span title=\"Goodreads Author!\">*<\/span>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.32 avg rating &mdash; 1,897 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2008\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer2612002765288265479\">For over half a century, scholars have laboured to show that C. S. Lewis\'s famed but apparently disorganised Chronicles of Narnia have an underlying symbolic coherence, pointing to such possible unifying themes as the seven sacraments, the seven dead<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText2612002765288265479\" style=\"display:none\">For over half a century, scholars have laboured to show that C. S. Lewis\'s famed but apparently disorganised Chronicles of Narnia have an underlying symbolic coherence, pointing to such possible unifying themes as the seven sacraments, the seven deadly sins, and the seven books of Spenser\'s Faerie Queene. None of these explanations has won general acceptance and the structure of Narnia\'s symbolism has remained a mystery.\n\nMichael Ward has finally solved the enigma. In Planet Narnia he demonstrates that medieval cosmology, a subject which fascinated Lewis throughout his life, provides the imaginative key to the seven novels. Drawing on the whole range of Lewis\'s writings (including previously unpublished drafts of the Chronicles), Ward reveals how the Narnia stories were designed to express the characteristics of the seven medieval planets - - Jupiter, Mars, Sol, Luna, Mercury, Venus, and Saturn - - planets which Lewis described as &quot;spiritual symbols of permanent value&quot; and &quot;especially worthwhile in our own generation.&quot; Using these seven symbols, Lewis secretly constructed the Chronicles so that in each book the plot-line, the ornamental details, and, most important, the portrayal of the Christ-figure of Aslan, all serve to communicate the governing planetary personality. The cosmological theme of each Chronicle is what Lewis called \'the kappa element in romance\', the atmospheric essence of a story, everywhere present but nowhere explicit. The reader inhabits this atmosphere and thus imaginatively gains connaitre knowledge of the spiritual character which the tale was created to embody.\n\nPlanet Narnia is a ground-breaking study that will provoke a major revaluation not only of the Chronicles, but of Lewis\'s whole literary and theological outlook. Ward uncovers a much subtler writer and thinker than has previously been recognized, whose central interests were hiddenness, immanence, and knowledge by acquaintance.&quot;<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"2612002765288265479\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div class=\'wtrButtonContainer wtrSignedOut\' At times almost forthrightly allegorical, at other times richly dreamlike (and indeed having a close connection to the symbolic world of dreams), this story of a young man who finds himself on a long journey through a land of fantasy is more truly the story of the spiritual quest that is at the core of his life\'s work, a quest that must end with the ultimate surrender of the self.\n\nThe glory of MacDonald\'s work is that this surrender is both hard won (or lost!) and yet rippling with joy when at last experienced. Chesterton\'s nutty agenda is really quite simple: to expose moral relativism and parlor nihilism for the devils he believes them to be. Focusing on \"heretics\" — those who pride themselves on their superiority to Christian views — Che<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText3916550489379969181\" style=\"display:none\">G. K. Chesterton, the &quot;Prince of Paradox,&quot; is at his witty best in this collection of twenty essays and articles from the turn of the twentieth century. Focusing on &quot;heretics&quot; — those who pride themselves on their superiority to Christian views — Chesterton appraises prominent figures who fall into that category from the literary and art worlds. Luminaries such as Rudyard Kipling, George Bernard Shaw, H. G. Wells, and James McNeill Whistler come under the author\'s scrutiny, where they meet with equal measures of his characteristic wisdom and good humor.In addition to incisive assessments of well-known individuals (&quot;Mr. Rudyard Kipling and Making the World Small&quot; and &quot;Mr. H. G. Wells and the Giants&quot;), these essays contain observations on the wider world. &quot;On Sandals and Simplicity,&quot; &quot;Science and the Savages,&quot; &quot;On Certain Modern Writers and the Institution of the Family,&quot; &quot;On Smart Novelists and the Smart Set,&quot; and &quot;Slum Novelists and the Slums&quot; reflect the main themes of Chesterton\'s life\'s work. Heretics roused the ire of some critics for censuring contemporary philosophies without providing alternatives; the author responded a few years later with a companion volume, Orthodoxy. Sardonic, jolly, and generous, both books are vintage Chesterton.\n\nHe is criticizing those who hold incomplete and inadequate views about &quot;life, the universe, and everything.&quot; He is, in short, criticizing all that host of non-Christian views of reality, as he demonstrated in his follow-up book Orthodoxy. The book is both an easy read and a difficult read. But he manages to demonstrate, among other things, that our new 21st century heresies are really not new because he himself deals with most of them.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"3916550489379969181\" Many critics complained of the book because it merely criticised current philosophies without offering any alternative philosophy. This book is an attempt to answer the challenge. It is the purpose of the writer to attempt an explanation, not of whether the Christian Faith can be believed, but of how he personally has come to believe it. The book is therefore arranged upon the positive principle of a riddle and its answer. It deals first with all the writer\'s own solitary and sincere speculations and then with the startling style in which they were all suddenly satisfied by the Christian Theology. Responding to the evolutionary materialism of his contemporary (and antagonist<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText1999540593945645996\" style=\"display:none\">What, if anything, is it that makes the human uniquely human? This, in part, is the question that G.K. Chesterton starts with in this classic exploration of human history. Responding to the evolutionary materialism of his contemporary (and antagonist) H.G. Wells, Chesterton in this work affirms human uniqueness and the unique message of the Christian faith. Writing in a time when social Darwinism was rampant, Chesterton instead argued that the idea that society has been steadily progressing from a state of primitivism and barbarity towards civilization is simply and flatly inaccurate. &quot;Barbarism and civilization were not successive stages in the progress of the world,&quot; he affirms, with arguments drawn from the histories of both Egypt and Babylon.  As always with Chesterton, there is in this analysis something (as he said of Blake) &quot;very plain and emphatic.&quot; He sees in Christianity a rare blending of philosophy and mythology, or reason and story, which satisfies both the mind and the heart. On both levels it rings true. As he puts it, &quot;in answer to the historical query of why it was accepted, and is accepted, I answer for millions of others in my reply; because it fits the lock; because it is like life.&quot; Here, as so often in Chesterton, we sense a lived, awakened faith. <span id="freeTextContainer3597079132122301338"><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the Goodreads database with this name.</i><br/><br/><br/>Clive Staples Lewis was one of the intellectual giants of the twentieth century and arguably one of the most influential writers of his day. He was a Fellow and Tutor in English Literature at Oxford University until 1954. He was unanimously elected to the Chair of Medieval and Renaissance Literature at Cambridge</span>
  <span id="freeText3597079132122301338" style="display:none"><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the Goodreads database with this name.</i><br /><br /><br />Clive Staples Lewis was one of the intellectual giants of the twentieth century and arguably one of the most influential writers of his day. He was a Fellow and Tutor in English Literature at Oxford University until 1954. He was unanimously elected to the Chair of Medieval and Renaissance Literature at Cambridge University, a position he held until his retirement. He wrote more than thirty books, allowing him to reach a vast audience, and his works continue to attract thousands of new readers every year. His most distinguished and popular accomplishments include <i>Mere Christianity</i>, <i>Out of the Silent Planet</i>, <i>The Great Divorce</i>, <i>The Screwtape Letters</i>, and the universally acknowledged classics <i>The Chronicles of Narnia</i>. To date, the Narnia books have sold over 100 million copies and been transformed into three major motion pictures.<br /><br />Lewis was married to poet <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/467360.Joy_Davidman" title="Joy Davidman" rel="nofollow noopener">Joy Davidman</a>.<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1988716._W_H__Lewis" title=" W.H. Lewis" rel="nofollow noopener"> W.H. Lewis</a> was his elder brother]</span>

It's a useful taste if one lives in England."<br />"How ever did you learn to do that, Mr. Denniston?" said Jane. "I don't think I should ever learn to like rain and snow."<br />"It's the other way round," said Denniston. "Everyone begins as a child by liking Weather. You learn the art of disliking it as you grow up. Noticed it on a snowy day? The grown-ups are all going about with long faces, but look at the children - and the dogs? They know what snow's made for."<br />"I'm sure I hated wet days as a child," said Jane.<br />"That's because the grown-ups kept you in," said Camilla.
